reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_layers: int, num_input_features: int, growth_rate: int):
        """
        :param num_layers: The number of layers in the dense block
        :param num_input_features: The number of input feature maps
        :param growth_rate: The growth rate for the dense block (new features added per layer)
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        layers = []
        for i in range(num_layers):
            layers.append(self._make_layer(num_input_features + i * growth_rate, growth_rate))
        self.layers = nn.ModuleList(layers)

    def _make_layer(self, in_features: int, growth_rate: int):
        """
        Creates a single layer with BatchNorm, ReLU, Conv2D, and Dropout.
        """
        return nn.Sequential(
            nn.BatchNorm2d(in_features),
            nn.ReLU(inplace=True),
            nn.Conv2d(in_features, growth_rate, kernel_size=3, padding=1, bias=False),
            nn.Dropout(0.0)
        )
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        :param x: Input tensor of shape (batch_size, num_input_features, height, width)
        :return: Concatenated output tensor with shape (batch_size, num_output_features, height, width)
        """
        features = [x]
        for layer in self.layers:
            new_feature = layer(x)
            features.append(new_feature)
            x = torch.cat(features, 1)  # Concatenate along channel axis
        return x
    
batch_size = 10
num_layers = 6
num_input_features = 32
growth_rate = 32
height, width = 224, 224

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, num_input_features, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_layers, num_input_features , growth_rate]

In the forward: ```python features = [x] for layer in self.layers: new_feature = layer(x) features.append(new_feature) x = torch.cat(features, 1) ``` So `x` is always the concatenation of ALL features so far. Each layer takes this `x` as input. Layer i: - input channels = num_input_features + i * growth_rate - output channels = growth_rate After layer i, x becomes num_input_features + (i+1) * growth_rate channels. Conv2d with kernel_size=3, padding=1 4. Dropout(0.0) - effectively no-op The key insight: we need to fuse operations. The reference does layer-by-layer with cat operations in between. This is memory bound because: 1. It creates many intermediate tensors 2. The cat operations copy data repeatedly Instead, we can write a custom CUDA kernel that: 1. Computes all outputs sequentially but fuses operations 2. Avoids repeated concatenation and re-reading by writing outputs directly to the final concatenated buffer However, since each layer depends on the concatenation of previous outputs, and each layer is a Conv2d (which needs all input channels), we can't easily parallelize across layers. But we CAN optimize within each layer by fusing BN -> ReLU -> Conv into a single kernel, and writing directly to the final output buffer. Wait, actually for each layer, the conv reads from ALL previous channels. So for layer 5, conv reads 192 channels. This is a lot of memory traffic if done naively. Let me think about this more carefully. In the reference: - Layer 0: BN/ReLU/Conv on 32 channels -> 32 channels. Then cat 32+32=64 - Layer 1: BN/ReLU/Conv on 64 channels -> 32 channels. Then cat 64+32=96 - ... After each layer, `x = torch.cat(features, 1)` creates a NEW tensor by copying all features. Then the next layer reads from this new tensor. This means: - Layer 0 output: 32 channels written - Cat: 32+32=64 channels written - Layer 1 reads 64 channels, writes 32 channels - Cat: 96 channels written - Layer 2 reads 96 channels, writes 32 channels - Cat: 128 channels written - ... Total memory traffic for cats alone: 64+96+128+160+192+224 = 864 channel-planes * H*W * 4 bytes Total memory traffic for layer reads: 32+64+96+128+160+192 = 672 channel-planes * H*W * 4 bytes With H=W=224, B=10: each channel-plane is 10*224*224*4 = ~2MB. 864 * 2MB = ~1.7GB of cat traffic alone! A fused approach can: 1. Allocate final output tensor of shape (B, 224, H, W) 2. Copy input to first 32 channels (or if in-place is safe, maybe not needed) 3. For each layer, read directly from the final output buffer's relevant channels, and write the new 32 channels to the next position in the same buffer This way, NO cat operations are needed! We just write sequentially to the output buffer. For each layer: - Input is channels [0, num_input_features + i*growth_rate) from the output buffer - Output is channels [num_input_features + i*growth_rate, num_input_features + (i+1)*growth_rate) For the conv operation, we still need to compute BN, ReLU, then Conv2d. We can fuse BN+ReLU into a preprocessing step (per-channel scale + shift, then relu), and then do the 3x3 conv. Given the channel sizes are manageable (max 192 input channels, 32 output channels), we have several options: 1. Use cuDNN for each layer (but this is what PyTorch does, and it's slow due to cat overhead) 2. Write a custom fused kernel For a custom kernel, the main challenge is that conv2d with 3x3 kernel and 192 input channels is compute-bound on H100. We could try to: - Fuse BN+ReLU into the conv via preprocessing, but conv still dominates - Maybe use im2col + GEMM approach in shared memory? Actually, let me think about whether we can approach this differently. Then compute 3x3 convolution (actually, the conv is applied AFTER ReLU, so it's a standard conv) Hmm, but the conv weights are 3x3. Actually 97B / 67T = 1.45ms. For FP32, TF32 tensor cores might be used but still, this is quite fast. However, the PyTorch reference likely spends a lot of time on memory traffic due to concatenation. But does bn(xi) create a view? No, it returns a new tensor. But after this, xi is a separate tensor, not out. Actually, we can do this in a much simpler way without custom CUDA: just preallocate the output and write slices. But the BN and conv still need to operate on the tensors. Let me check if this is faster: ```python out = torch.empty(B, 224, H, W, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) out[:, :32] = x for i, layer in enumerate(self.layers): bn, relu, conv, _ = layer in_ch = 32 + i*32 # bn operates on out[:, :in_ch] - but we need to avoid modifying out in-place if we care about input for next iteration # Actually after BN, the values change, but that's fine since we only need the conv output temp = bn(out[:, :in_ch]) temp = relu(temp) new_feat = conv(temp) out[:, in_ch:in_ch+32] = new_feat ``` Wait, but `bn(out[:, :in_ch])` doesn't modify `out` in-place unless we do it that way. BN in eval mode is just a pointwise affine transform, so it doesn't matter if we create a temporary. But actually this approach still calls cuDNN conv for each layer, which is quite fast. The main win is avoiding `torch.cat` which creates a new tensor and copies all previous features each time. Is this faster enough? Let me think. In the reference, after each layer they do `x = torch.cat(features, 1)`. This creates NEW tensors of size 64, 96, 128, 160, 192, 224 channels. The concatenation copies all previous feature maps. In our modified version, we just write the new 32 channels to a preallocated buffer - no copying of old data. But wait, is there an even better custom CUDA approach? However, the instruction says "Write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces a PyTorch reference". We need a custom CUDA kernel to get the best speedup. Let me think about a fully fused kernel. For each pixel position (b, h, w), we need to: 1. For layer 0: read C0=32 input channels, apply BN+ReLU, then compute 3x3 conv -> write 32 channels 2. For layer 1: read C1=64 channels (original 32 + layer0 32), apply BN+ReLU, then 3x3 conv -> write 32 channels 3. ... Since each layer's conv depends on a 3x3 neighborhood, and each subsequent layer depends on the outputs of previous layers, we can't easily process all layers for a single pixel independently. But we CAN process tiles of the image. For a tile, we compute layer 0, then layer 1, etc., keeping all intermediate results in shared memory or registers. But the channel counts get large. Actually, for H=224, W=224, if we process the image in tiles, say 16x16 output tiles with 18x18 input tiles (for 3x3 conv), and we have 192 input channels max, the shared memory needed for activations would be 18*18*192*4 bytes = ~248KB, which exceeds shared memory. We could use smaller tiles. Or we could load channels in chunks. Alternatively, since the conv is compute-bound, maybe we should just let cuDNN do the conv but avoid cats. Hmm, but the task specifically asks for a custom CUDA kernel. Wait, I realize there's another approach. We can write a kernel that for each layer, fuses BN+ReLU+Conv, where the conv part is a simple direct convolution (not im2col/GEMM). With 32 output channels and up to 192 input channels, for each output pixel we need 192*3*3*32 = 55K multiply-adds. That might be OK if we process enough pixels in parallel. Actually, I think a tile-based approach where each block computes a tile of all output channels for one layer would work. But we need to chain 6 layers together... Let me reconsider. The simplest custom CUDA kernel that helps is just replacing the entire forward pass with: 1. Allocate output tensor 2. Copy input 3. For each layer, launch a kernel that reads from output, applies BN, ReLU, and direct conv, writes back to output We can also try to launch multiple kernels but the overhead of 6 kernel launches is negligible compared to the compute. For the conv, since in_channels varies from 32 to 192 and out_channels is always 32, we have a few options: 1. For each configuration, compile a templated kernel 2. Use a generic kernel with dynamic sizes Option 1 is better for performance. We can compile kernels for in_channels = 32, 64, 96, 128, 160, 192, out_channels = 32. For each kernel, we can do a direct convolution. Let's think about the layout. - Input: [B, in_channels, H, W] - Weights: [out_channels, in_channels, 3, 3] - BN params: [in_channels] for mean, var, weight, bias For each output position (b, oc, h, w): sum = 0 for ic in range(in_channels): scale = bn_weight[ic] / sqrt(bn_var[ic] + eps) bias = bn_bias[ic] - bn_mean[ic] * scale for kh in range(3): for kw in range(3): ih = h + kh - 1 iw = w + kw - 1 val = input[b, ic, ih, iw] val = val * scale + bias val = max(val, 0) sum += val * weight[oc, ic, kh, kw] output[b, oc, h, w] = sum This fuses BN, ReLU, and Conv into one read of input and one write of output. No intermediate tensors! The problem is arithmetic intensity. For each output pixel (10*224*224*32 = ~16M output values total per layer), we do in_channels * 9 multiplies. The input read is 10*224*224*in_channels but with 3x3 neighborhood, it's about 9x more reads. Wait no, each input element is read by multiple output pixels. For 224x224 with 3x3 kernel, each input element is read by up to 9 output elements. So total memory reads = ~9 * B * H * W * in_channels. Total compute = B * H * W * out_channels * in_channels * 9. Arithmetic intensity = (B*H*W*out*in*9) / (B*H*W*in*9 * 4 bytes) = out / 4 = 32/4 = 8 FLOPs/byte. This is compute-bound! At 67 TFLOPS and 3.35 TB/s, the compute-bound threshold is ~20 FLOPs/byte. So 8 FLOPs/byte is actually memory-bound! Wait, let me recalculate. Each multiply-add is 2 FLOPs. So compute = B * H * W * out * in * 9 * 2 = 501,760 * 32 * 192 * 9 * 2 = 55.5B FLOPs for layer 5. Memory = we need to read all input elements in the receptive field. For interior pixels, each output reads 3*3*in elements. Total input reads = B * H * W * in * 9 * 4 bytes = 501,760 * 192 * 9 * 4 = 3.47GB. But wait, with good caching on GPU, the 3x3 neighborhood is heavily cached. Each input element is reused by ~9 output pixels. So actual DRAM traffic is closer to B * H * W * in * 4 bytes (reading each input once from DRAM) plus weights in * out * 9 * 4 bytes, plus output writes B * H * W * out * 4 bytes. If we assume perfect cache for the 3x3 neighborhood, then memory traffic is: - Input: B * H * W * in * 4 = 501,760 * 192 * 4 = 385MB - Weights: in * out * 3 * 3 * 4 = 192 * 32 * 9 * 4 = 221KB - Output: B * H * W * out * 4 = 501,760 * 32 * 4 = 64MB Total: ~450MB Compute: 55.5B FLOPs Arithmetic intensity: 55.5B / 450MB = 123 FLOPs/byte. This is compute-bound! Wait, I need to be more careful. With a 3x3 kernel, the input tile for each output tile has halo regions. For a 16x16 output tile, we need an 18x18 input tile. The expansion factor is (18/16)^2 = 1.265x. So actual DRAM reads are ~1.265x the input size, not 9x. And with good cache behavior, it's ~1.265x. But for a naive direct convolution where each thread reads its own 3x3 patch without sharing, we'd get 9x reads. That's bad. So we should use shared memory to load input tiles. Each block loads an 18x18 tile (or whatever halo size) into shared memory, then threads read from shared memory. Given that the channel count is large (up to 192), we should process multiple output channels per block and load input channels in chunks. Actually, let me think about a more practical implementation. On H100, we have tensor cores and very fast L2 cache. A simple direct conv might actually perform surprisingly well if we process enough pixels in parallel. But tensor cores require specific data formats. Actually, wait. We know that PyTorch's nn.Conv2d uses cuDNN, which is highly optimized. If we just call conv2d 6 times but avoid the cat copies, we'd eliminate: - 6 cat operations copying 864 channel-planes total - The overhead of creating intermediate concatenated tensors But we still have 6 separate conv calls, each going through cuDNN. cuDNN is very fast for these sizes. Can a custom direct conv beat cuDNN? Unlikely for individual conv layers. cuDNN uses winograd, tensor cores, etc. For 3x3 conv with 192 input and 32 output channels, cuDNN is likely extremely fast. So the REAL win is avoiding the concatenation overhead. The computation itself is likely close to optimal with cuDNN. Therefore, the best strategy is probably: 1. Write a custom kernel that does fused BN+ReLU+Conv for small input channel counts where direct conv can match or beat cuDNN overhead 2. OR, write a kernel that just copies the input to output and then uses cuDNN for each conv but without cat Wait, since we need a "custom CUDA kernel", maybe we can do: - A custom kernel that just does the concat-avoidance wrapper, plus fuses BN+ReLU into a preprocessing kernel, then calls cuDNN conv? No, we can't call cuDNN from custom CUDA in PyTorch easily without going through ATen. Actually, we CAN call PyTorch ops from within our model. The task just says implement `forward()` with a custom CUDA kernel. It doesn't say the ENTIRE forward must be one kernel. We could have multiple kernels. But actually, the simplest approach that might be very effective: 1. Use PyTorch conv2d (cuDNN) on the preprocessed slice 3. Write result directly to output tensor This avoids cats entirely. But is this "custom CUDA kernel"? For each layer, since we need BN+ReLU on the input slice, and then conv, and we want to avoid creating temporary tensors... Wait, PyTorch's nn.Conv2d takes a tensor and creates a new tensor. We can't easily use the output tensor as the conv output buffer because conv expects contiguous channels in a specific layout. So: ```python out = torch.empty(B, 224, H, W, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype, memory_format=torch.channels_last) out[:, :32] = x x_temp = x for i, layer in enumerate(self.layers): bn, relu, conv, _ = layer in_ch = 32 + i*32 # BN + ReLU is just transform x_temp = bn(out[:, :in_ch]) x_temp = relu(x_temp) # Conv writes directly to the final output conv_out = conv(x_temp) out[:, in_ch:in_ch+32] = conv_out ``` This avoids cats, but x_temp is still a temporary. However, in eval mode, BN+ReLU is bandwidth-bound and the temporary is small-ish. The main issue is that `bn()` creates a new tensor, then `relu()` creates another, then `conv()` creates another. That's 3 temporary allocations per layer. Wait, does `bn(out[:, :in_ch])` share memory? No, BatchNorm creates a new tensor. Actually, for maximum performance, I think the best approach is: **Option A: Pure PyTorch optimization** Preallocate output, use slices, rely on cuDNN for conv. This avoids cat copies completely. Might be 2-3x faster. **Option B: Custom fused BN+ReLU+Conv kernel** Write a direct conv kernel that reads input, applies BN+ReLU on the fly, and computes the conv. This avoids ALL intermediate allocations and fuses the memory-bound BN+ReLU into the conv's memory reads. For 192 input channels, if each thread computes one output pixel, it needs to accumulate IN_CH * 9 values. For 192 * 9 = 1728 multiplies per pixel. If they all read input at (b, ic, h0+h+kh, w0+w+kw), then adjacent threads read adjacent memory (since they differ in w). This is coalesced. But each thread loops over ic from 0 to 191 and kh, kw from 0 to 2. For each (ic, kh, kw), all threads read the same channel ic but different spatial positions. These reads are coalesced if the spatial layout is NCHW (channels first). Yes! In NCHW, the memory layout is: for a given batch b and channel c, all H*W pixels are contiguous. So adjacent threads reading adjacent w are reading contiguous memory. Perfect coalescing. So if we have 256 threads in a block, and they all read the same (b, ic, kh, kw) pattern but different spatial offsets, they read 256 contiguous floats. That's one memory transaction (or a few) per (ic, kh, kw) step. For 192 input channels * 9 kernel positions, that's 1728 steps. Each step reads 256 floats from global memory. The L2 cache is 50MB. The total working set for one layer is 392MB, which is larger than L2. However, the cache works by reusing recently accessed lines. If we process spatial tiles in order, adjacent tiles for the same batch/channel are nearby in memory and might stay in cache. For a 16x16 output tile, adjacent tiles share a lot of data. If we process tiles in row-major order, the cache might hold the data for recent tiles. But with 196 tiles and 32 output channels being computed concurrently (interleaved by blocks), it depends on execution order. Actually, on GPU, blocks are scheduled dynamically. It's hard to guarantee spatial locality. To ensure good caching, we should use shared memory for the input tile. With 256 threads, each thread computes 1 pixel. This makes a lot of sense! And occupancy: - Shared memory per block: ~85KB - Blocks per SM: 228KB / 85KB = 2 blocks (with some headroom). - Threads per block: 256 - Concurrent threads per SM: 2 * 256 = 512 - Max threads per SM: 2048 - Occupancy: 25%. If we process 2 output channels per block, each with an 8x8 spatial tile: - Total pixels: 2 * 8 * 8 = 128. Actually, let me step back and think about whether we can just use a simple matmul-based approach. Since the conv is 3x3, we can compute it as a direct dot product. The key is to unroll the kernel loops. For each thread computing one output pixel at (b, oc, h, w): ```cpp float acc = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < IN_CH; ic++) { float scale = bn_weight[ic]; float bias = bn_bias[ic]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int ih = h + kh - 1; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int iw = w + kw - 1; float val = input[(b * IN_CH + ic) * H * W + ih * W + iw]; val = val * scale + bias; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); acc += val * weight[((oc * IN_CH + ic) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]; } } } output[...] = acc; ``` This does BN+ReLU inline with each input read. No temporary! But we need to know if this compiles and runs fast enough. The memory reads are: for each (ic, kh, kw), all threads read different val from global memory. Since adjacent threads have adjacent w (if we map threads to w first), reads are coalesced. For IN=192 and 3x3, the inner loop has 1728 iterations. We can unroll the 3x3 loops completely. For the channel loop, we might unroll by a factor but not completely (192 is large). With weights in shared memory or registers? Each thread needs weights for its output channel. If each block handles one output channel, all threads share weights. So we can preload the 1728 weights per output channel into shared memory. Actually, this is a good approach. Let me formalize: ```cpp template<int IN_CH> __global__ void dense_layer_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ inp, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, int B, int H, int W, int out_ch_begin, int out_ch_end ) { int oc = blockIdx.z; // output channel int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y / (W / TILE_H) * TILE_H + threadIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.y % (W / TILE_H) * TILE_H + threadIdx.x; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; // Load weights into shared memory __shared__ float wshared[IN_CH * 9]; for (int i = threadIdx.y * TILE_H + threadIdx.x; i < IN_CH * 9; i += TILE_H * TILE_H) { wshared[i] = weight[((oc * IN_CH) * 9) + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < IN_CH; ic++) { float scale = bn_weight[ic]; float bias = bn_bias[ic]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int ih = h + kh - 1; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int iw = w + kw - 1; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { float val = inp[((b * IN_CH + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; val = val * scale + bias; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); acc += val * wshared[ic * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } } int out_C = /* total output channels, maybe passed as arg */; out[((b * out_C + out_ch_begin + oc) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } ``` Wait, mapping blockIdx.y to spatial tiles is tricky. Let me think about grid dimensions. If TILE is the spatial tile size in each dimension: - gridDim.x = B - gridDim.y = (H / TILE) * (W / TILE) - gridDim.z = OUT_CH Inside the block: - blockDim.x = TILE - blockDim.y = TILE Then for a given block: - b = blockIdx.x - tile_idx = blockIdx.y - tile_h = tile_idx / (W / TILE) * TILE - tile_w = tile_idx % (W / TILE) * TILE - h = tile_h + threadIdx.y - w = tile_w + threadIdx.x This is clean. Each block computes a TILE x TILE spatial region for one output channel. For H=W=224, TILE=16: - Blocks y = 14 * 14 = 196 - Total blocks = 10 * 196 * 32 = 62,720 per layer. Shared memory: IN_CH * 9 * 4 bytes. For IN=192: 192 * 9 * 4 = 6.9KB. Very small! No shared memory for activations. Register usage: each thread needs at most a few registers for indices, plus the 1728 iterations of multiply-add. The compiler can optimize this. Actually, with TILE=16 and block size 256 threads, and no shared memory for activations, occupancy is great. We can have up to 32 blocks per SM (limited by thread count: 2048/256 = 8 blocks per SM). So 8 blocks * 62,720 total = good occupancy. But wait, without caching activations in shared memory, will we be memory bound? As I calculated, with good L2 cache, the total unique input reads are about the size of the input tensor once. Since different blocks for the same output channel read the same input but compute different output positions, and blocks for different output channels also read the same input, the L2 cache should help a lot. On H100, L2 cache is 50MB. The input for one layer is at most 385MB (for IN=192, B=10). That's larger than L2, but not all of it needs to be cached simultaneously. A block reads roughly 18*18*192 = 62K floats = 248KB. Many blocks can run concurrently on an SM (8 blocks = 2MB of working set). This fits well in L2. I think this kernel design is worth trying. It's simple and likely to provide good speedup just by avoiding concat temporaries, plus it fuses BN+ReLU into the conv memory reads. But there might be an even better approach. We could process all 6 layers in a way that keeps intermediate results in registers/shared memory between layers for small tiles. But that requires very large shared memory because the channel count grows. For a 16x16 tile, layer 5 needs 192 channels, which is 248KB of fp32 - too much. Even for 8x8 tiles, it's 10*10*192*4 = 76.8KB. We could store that. But across 6 layers, we'd need to store all intermediate features somewhere. The concatenated output needs 224 channels * 8*8 = 14.3KB. Actually, we can store the full concatenated features for the tile in shared memory or just write to global memory and read back. Since the output buffer is the same as the input buffer for subsequent layers (with a growing channel count), writing to global memory and reading back is natural. The H100 has very high memory bandwidth, and with L2 cache, reading back recently written data is fast. So the multi-kernel approach is fine. Let me also think about whether we can use Tensor Cores. FP32 Tensor Cores are available on H100 but require specific data formats and operations. For a 3x3 conv, it's not trivial to map to tensor cores without im2col or Winograd. No, 512 is fine. - Shared memory: 2 * IN * 9 * 4 = 13.8KB for IN=192. - Block count: 10 * 14 * 14 * 16 = 31,360. Let's analyze: - Each block computes 8 * 8 * 8 = 512 output values. - Total blocks per layer: 31,360. - Each block loads 8 * IN * 9 weights = 55KB (for IN=192). - Each thread computes 1 output value by looping over IN channels and doing 9 multiply-adds per channel. - Since there are 512 threads, we have 512 parallel loads for activations. But adjacent threads have adjacent spatial positions. Wait, with 512 threads and 8x8 spatial tile, 8 output channels: there are 64 spatial positions and 8 channels. How do we map threads? - threadIdx.x % 8 = channel offset within the 8 - threadIdx.x / 8 = spatial index (0..63) - spatial index / 8 = h offset - spatial index % 8 = w offset Then thread (c, h, w) computes output channel (oc_base + c) at position (h, w). For loading weights: 8 output channels * 192 input channels * 9 = 13,824 weights. Each thread loads ~27 weights. We can have all threads participate in loading weights into shared memory. For activation reads: all 512 threads read different spatial positions but possibly the same input channels. In NCHW layout, for a given channel ic and batch b, the 64 spatial positions are contiguous. But 512 threads reading 64 spatial positions each? No, each thread reads 192*9 = 1728 spatial positions (different channels, same relative offsets). For a fixed (ic, kh, kw), all threads read the same channel but different spatial positions. Since there are only 64 unique spatial positions per channel per block, and 512 threads, many threads share the same spatial position. Specifically, 8 threads (for 8 output channels) share the same spatial input position. These 8 threads should be grouped together for good coalescing... but they all read the same address! That's a broadcast, not coalesced. Oh no, I made a mistake. If threads are mapped as (c, spatial), then for a given spatial position, 8 threads (different c) read the SAME input address. This results in 8-way bank conflicts if in shared memory, or just 8 threads reading the same global memory address simultaneously. On modern GPUs, broadcast reads from global memory are handled reasonably well (via L1 cache), but it's not ideal. Alternative mapping: map threads to spatial positions first, with each thread handling all 8 output channels. - threadIdx.x = spatial index (0..63) - Each thread computes 8 output channels for its spatial position. - Thread loads input value once, applies BN+ReLU, then multiplies by 8 different weights and accumulates into 8 registers. This is much better! - For each (ic, kh, kw), 64 threads read 64 adjacent input floats (coalesced). - Each thread does 8 multiply-adds with 8 different weights. - Weights for the 8 output channels are loaded into shared memory (or registers). - Each thread needs 8 accumulators (registers). This is excellent! Let's formalize: ```cpp template<int IN_CH, int OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK> __global__ void dense_layer_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ inp, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, int B, int H, int W, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE = 8; const int SPATIAL_PER_BLOCK = TILE * TILE; // 64 int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_h = (blockIdx.y / (W / TILE)) * TILE; int tile_w = (blockIdx.y % (W / TILE)) * TILE; int oc_base = blockIdx.z * OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK; int tid = threadIdx.x; // 0..63 int h = tile_h + tid / TILE; int w = tile_w + tid % TILE; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; // Load weights for OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK output channels __shared__ float wshared[OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK * IN_CH * 9]; int total_weights = OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK * IN_CH * 9; for (int i = tid; i < total_weights; i += SPATIAL_PER_BLOCK) { wshared[i] = weight[((oc_base * IN_CH) * 9) + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc[OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK; c++) acc[c] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < IN_CH; ic++) { float scale = bn_weight[ic]; float bias = bn_bias[ic]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int ih = h + kh - 1; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int iw = w + kw - 1; float val = 0.0f; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { val = inp[((b * IN_CH + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; val = val * scale + bias; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); } #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK; c++) { float w = wshared[((c * IN_CH + ic) * 9) + kh * 3 + kw]; acc[c] += val * w; } } } } #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK; c++) { int oc = oc_base + c; out[((b * (out_ch_begin + OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK * gridDim.z ? ... ) * H + h) * W + w] = acc[c]; } } ``` Wait, I need to pass the total output channels or just compute the output index correctly. For the kernel, the output is written to `out` which is the full output tensor. The output channel index for this layer is `out_ch_begin + oc`. Since `out` has shape [B, total_C, H, W], the offset is `((b * total_C) + out_ch_begin + oc) * H * W + h * W + w`. But `total_C` is a constant = num_input_features + num_layers * growth_rate = 32 + 6*32 = 224. We can pass it as a parameter. Also, IN_CH varies per layer. OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK should be a template parameter or constant. Since OUT=32always, and we process in blocks, we could have OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK = 8, so 4 z-blocks per layer. Actually wait, OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK=8 and OUT=32 means gridDim.z = 32/8 = 4. Yes. For layer 0: IN=32, OUT=32. For layer 1: IN=64, OUT=32. ... For layer 5: IN=192, OUT=32. We can create 6 kernels or one generic kernel. Given we're using `load_inline`, we can either: 1. Write 6 kernel functions, one for each layer's IN_CH 2. For approach 2, the compiler might still optimize the loop if IN_CH is passed as a `const int` parameter, but unrolling won't happen. But inside CUDA, if we do: ```cpp switch(in_ch) { case 32: loop<32>(); break; case 64: loop<64>(); break; ... } ``` This works if we template the inner loop. Let me design a cleaner generic kernel helper. Actually, PyTorch's ATen ops are available in load_inline CUDA. We can also write device functions with templates. Let me think about a practical implementation: ```cpp template<int IN_CH, int OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK> __device__ __forceinline__ void compute_layer( float* out, const float* inp, const float* weight, const float* bn_weight, const float* bn_bias, int B, int H, int W, int out_C, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE = 8; const int SPATIAL = TILE * TILE; int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_idx = blockIdx.y; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; int oc_base = blockIdx.z * OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK; int tid = threadIdx.x; int h = tile_h + tid / TILE; int w = tile_w + tid % TILE; if (h >= H || w >= W) return; __shared__ float wshared[OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK * IN_CH * 9]; int total_w = OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK * IN_CH * 9; for (int i = tid; i < total_w; i += SPATIAL) { wshared[i] = weight[((oc_base * IN_CH) * 9) + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc[OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK; c++) acc[c] = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IN_CH; ic++) { float scale = bn_weight[ic]; float bias = bn_bias[ic]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { int ih = h + kh - 1; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int iw = w + kw - 1; float val = 0.0f; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { val = inp[((b * IN_CH + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; val = val * scale + bias; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); } #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK; c++) { acc[c] += val * wshared[((c * IN_CH + ic) * 9) + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } } int out_offset_base = ((b * out_C + out_ch_begin) * H + h) * W + w; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK; c++) { out[out_offset_base + c * H * W] = acc[c]; } } ``` Then for each IN_CH: ```cpp template<int OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK=8> __global__ void dense_layer_32(...) { compute_layer<32, OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK>(...); } __global__ void dense_layer_64(...) { compute_layer<64, OUT_CH_PER_BLOCK>(...); } ... ``` This is clean. Now, the batch norm parameters: in PyTorch, BatchNorm stores `running_mean`, `running_var`, `weight`, `bias`. The formula is: `y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias` We need to precompute scale = weight / sqrt(var + eps) and shift = bias - mean * scale, and pass these to the kernel. We do this on the CPU side in PyTorch before launching the kernel. In `ModelNew.forward()`: 1. Allocate output tensor 2. Copy input to output[:, :32] 3. For each layer i: - Extract BN params, compute scale and shift - Launch kernel with IN=32+i*32 - Grid: (B, num_tiles, OUT/8) - Input: output[:, :IN] - Output: output[:, IN:IN+32] Wait, there's a subtle issue. The input to layer i is `output[:, :IN]`. But `output` is being written to incrementally. For layer i, channels [0, IN) are already computed (by previous layers or by initial copy). The kernel reads from these channels and writes to channels [IN, IN+32). There's no race condition because reads and writes are to disjoint channel ranges. But what about within a layer? For a given layer, the conv output doesn't depend on itself, so no issue. Also, after writing to `output[:, IN:IN+32]`, the next layer will read from `output[:, :IN+32]`. This works perfectly! One more optimization: instead of having `if (ih >= 0 ...)` checks inside the inner loop, we could pad the input tensor with zeros. But that requires pre-padding or modifying the tensor. Actually, PyTorch's conv with padding=1 handles this automatically. In our kernel, for h=0, ih=-1 is out of bounds, so we need the checks. But with TILE=8 and h ranging from 0 to 223, the boundary conditions only happen for tiles on the edges. Most blocks (interior) don't hit boundaries. However, the `if` check adds overhead. Alternatively, we can clamp the indices, but that's still a branch. For performance, we might unroll the spatial tile and handle boundaries separately. But for now, the `if` is fine if most warps don't diverge too much. Actually, for a tile on the top edge, ALL threads in the block have h=0 and thus ih=-1 for kh=0. That's uniform divergence within the warp (all threads take the same branch), which is efficient on GPUs. Let me think about another issue: occupancy and register usage. Each thread has `acc[8]` = 8 floats = 8 registers. Plus loop variables, indices, and 8 weights loaded temporarily plus the activation value. Total maybe 32-64 registers per thread. With 256 threads per block (SPATIAL=64 but we might use 256 threads for loading weights faster), wait... I said SPATIAL = 64, but what if we use 256 threads per block? Then only 64 threads do computation, and the other 192 help load weights. Or we can just use 64 threads per block. With 64 threads per block and say 32 registers per thread = 2048 registers per block. With 64K registers per SM, we can have 32 blocks. With shared memory of ~55KB (for IN=192), we can have 4 blocks. So shared memory is the limiting factor. If shared memory is 55KB, blocks per SM = 4. Threads per SM = 4 * 64 = 256. Occupancy = 256/2048 = 12.5%. That's low. Can we reduce shared memory? We don't actually NEED shared memory for weights if we're willing to read them from global memory. Weight reads are broadcast (all threads in a block read the same weight at the same time in the inner loop). On H100, broadcast from global memory is cached in L1. Since we're reading weights sequentially and each weight is used by all threads, it should stay in L1. If we remove shared memory for weights: - Memory reads: each thread reads each weight once per iteration. For 8 output channels, that's 8 weights per (ic, kh, kw). With 64 threads, all reading 8 different weights, these are contiguous in memory (since weights are [OUT, IN, 3, 3]). For a fixed ic, kh, kw, the weights for the 8 output channels are contiguous! So 64 threads each reading 8 contiguous floats = 512 floats. These read 8 64-byte cache lines or 4 128-byte cache lines. With coalescing, this is efficient. Actually, for output channels oc_base to oc_base+7, and fixed ic, kh, kw: weight indices are `((oc_base + c) * IN + ic) * 9 + kh*3 +kw` for c=0..7. These are contiguous because `c` varies by 1 and `IN*9` is the stride. Wait, the stride between output channels is `IN*9`, not 1. So the weights for different output channels are NOT contiguous! They are `IN*9` floats apart. Reading `w[0], w[IN*9], w[2*IN*9], ...` is strided by `IN*9` * 4 bytes. For IN=192, that's 6912 bytes apart. This is terrible for coalescing and caching. So shared memory for weights IS important. Alternative: transpose the weight layout to [IN, OUT, 3, 3] or [IN, 3, 3, OUT]. Or keep it as is but load one output channel's weights at a time into a shared memory buffer. Wait, if we process 1 output channel per block (instead of 8), then shared memory is only IN*9*4 = 6.9KB. Block count = B * tiles * OUT = 10*28*28*32 = 250,880 blocks. Each with 64 threads, 6.9KB shared memory. Blocks per SM = 228/6.9 = 33, but limited to 32 by hardware. Concurrent threads = 32 * 64 = 2048. That's 100% occupancy! And with 1 output channel per block, weight loading is IN*9 per block. Input reads are the same. The only downside is that each block does less work (1 output channel instead of 8), but the occupancy is much higher. Let me compare: - 8-output blocks: 31,360 blocks, 55KB shared memory, 4 blocks/SM, 256 threads/SM -> 12.5% occupancy - 1-output blocks: 250,880 blocks, 6.9KB shared memory, 32 blocks/SM, 2048 threads/SM -> 100% occupancy The 1-output configuration will likely be faster due to much better latency hiding through occupancy. With 100% occupancy, when one block is waiting for memory, another can execute. But wait, with 1-output blocks, do we have enough warps to hide latency? Yes, 2048 threads = 64 warps per SM. With independent warps, this provides excellent latency hiding. And the total number of blocks (250,880) is plenty to saturate all SMs. Let me verify: 250,880 blocks / 132 SMs = 1900 blocks per SM. With 32 blocks executing concurrently, that's 60 waves. More than enough. So I think 1 output channel per block with 64 threads (8x8 tile) is the way to go. Or even 1 output channel with 256 threads (16x16 tile)? With 16x16 tile, 256 threads: - Shared memory: still 6.9KB. - Blocks per SM: 32 (shared memory limit) or 8 (thread limit: 2048/256=8). Thread limit wins. So 8 blocks/SM, 256 threads each = 2048 threads = 100% occupancy. - Block count: 10*14*14*32 = 62,720. - Threads do more work per block (256 pixels instead of 64). 16x16 tile seems better because each block does more contiguous work, reducing the number of blocks launched and better amortizing block overhead. And we still get 100% occupancy because thread count limits us to 8 blocks, which is within the 32 block and shared memory limits. Actually, with 16x16 tile and 256 threads, each block computes 256 output values. Total blocks = 62,720. Per SM blocks = 62,720 / 132 = 475. With 8 concurrent blocks, that's 60 waves. Good. For memory coalescing with 16x16 tile: 256 threads reading adjacent spatial positions? We need to map them correctly. If thread idx maps to (h_offset, w_offset) with h varying faster or w varying faster? In NCHW, pixels are stored with w as the fastest varying dimension within a row. So for a given channel and row h, pixels w=0..W-1 are contiguous. If we map: - tid/16 = h_offset (0..15) - tid%16 = w_offset (0..15) Then for a fixed h_offset, 16 threads read 16 contiguous pixels. For different h_offsets, threads read pixels offset by W*16 floats. If W=224, that's 3584 bytes apart. These might be in different cache lines. The memory controller can handle multiple cache lines simultaneously. Actually, for coalescing, it's ideal if threads in a warp read contiguous memory. A warp is 32 threads. If the first warp (tid 0..31) covers h=0..1 and w=0..15, then: - Threads 0..15 read row 0, w=0..15: contiguous, 16 floats = 64 bytes. - Threads 16..31 read row 1, w=0..15: contiguous but offset by W floats = 896 bytes from row 0. A memory transaction is 32 bytes or 128 bytes. If transaction size is 128 bytes, then threads 0..31 read two separate 64-byte segments, which fits in one or two 128-byte transactions if the rows are aligned properly. Actually, row 0 starts at offset 0 (aligned). Row 1 starts at offset W=224 floats = 896 bytes. 896 / 128 = 7. So it's 128-byte aligned. Great! Both rows start at 128-byte boundaries. Each warp's read requires 2 128-byte transactions. This is good. For a 32-thread warp reading 2 rows of 16 pixels each, total read is 32 floats = 128 bytes. But they come from 2 different 128-byte cache lines (row 0: bytes 0-63, row 1: bytes 896-959). So 2 memory transactions. Still efficient. And with 256 threads, we have 8 warps per block. For even better coalescing, we could process 1x32 or 32x1 strips. But 16x16 tiles probably have good cache behavior for 3x3 convolution because the 18x18 input region is relatively compact. Actually, let me reconsider tile shape. For a 16x16 tile with 3x3 kernel, the input is 18x18. Total input size = 324 pixels. With 256 threads, each warp might load different parts. But if threads just read on demand in the loop, the L1/L2 cache handles it. Actually, we can load the 18x18 input tile into shared memory! For one channel at a time: - 18*18 = 324 floats = 1.3KB. With 256 threads, each thread loads 1-2 floats. Very efficient. - Then all threads read from shared memory (no bank conflicts if we structure it right). Wait, if we load the input tile for one channel into shared memory, and process all 9 kernel positions, then for the next channel we load the next tile. This would DRAMATICALLY reduce global memory reads and improve performance. For a 16x16 output tile, input is 18x18. - Shared memory per channel tile: 18*18*4 = 1.3KB. - Process all 9 kernel positions by reading from shared memory. - Then load next channel. This is much better than reading from global memory 9 times per channel! It reduces memory bandwidth by 9x for activations. Let's compare with the no-shared-memory approach. In both cases, total unique input per block is 18*18*IN. But without shared memory, each thread reads its own 3x3 neighborhood, so total reads are 256*9*IN, but with heavy cache reuse. With shared memory, we explicitly load 18*18*IN once, and then read from shared memory 256*9*IN times. Shared memory is much faster. So shared memory for activations is a huge win! But can we fit both weights and activations in shared memory? - Activations: 18*18*4 = 1.3KB per channel. We load one channel at a time. - Weights: 6.9KB (for IN=192, but we only need one output channel's weights, which is IN*9*4). Total shared memory: ~8.2KB. Very small! And we only need 256 threads to load the 324 activation values and 1728 weight values. 256 threads can load all weights in ~7 iterations (1728/256 = 6.75). So the ideal kernel is: ```cpp template<int IN_CH> __global__ void dense_layer_kernel(...) { // Block: 256 threads // Computes 16x16 spatial tile for 1 output channel __shared__ float wshared[IN_CH * 9]; __shared__ float xshared[18 * 18]; // for one channel // Load weights for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < IN_CH * 9; i += 256) { wshared[i] = weight[oc * IN_CH * 9 + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IN_CH; ic++) { // Load 18x18 input tile for channel ic into xshared // 324 values, 256 threads -> 2 iterations for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 18*18; i += 256) { int ih = i / 18 + tile_h - 1; int iw = i % 18 + tile_w - 1; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { xshared[i] = inp[((b * IN_CH + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } else { xshared[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Apply BN+ReLU in shared memory or on the fly float scale = bn_weight[ic]; float bias = bn_bias[ic]; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int ih = h + kh - 1 - (tile_h - 1); int iw = w + kw - 1 - (tile_w - 1); float val = xshared[ih * 18 + iw]; val = val * scale + bias; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); acc += val * wshared[ic * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } __syncthreads(); // before loading next channel } // write output } ``` Wait, for the BN+ReLU, we apply it to the activation value after reading from shared memory. We could pre-process the entire shared memory tile: ```cpp for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 18*18; i += 256) { xshared[i] = xshared[i] * scale + bias; xshared[i] = fmaxf(xshared[i], 0.0f); } __syncthreads(); ``` This adds a parallel loop but might be faster than doing it per-thread in the 3x3 loop. But actually, with only 324 elements and 256 threads, it's essentially free. However, we need to be careful: the boundary pixels loaded as 0.0f should stay 0.0f after BN+ReLU if we process them. If ih or iw is out of bounds, we loaded 0.0f. Then `0.0f * scale + bias = bias`, and `fmaxf(bias, 0.0f)` is not zero if bias < 0! But padding should be 0, not `fmaxf(bias, 0)`. Ah! This is an important point. In conv with padding=1, out-of-bounds input is treated as 0. If we apply BN+ReLU to a 0 padding pixel, we get `fmaxf(bias, 0)`, not 0. This is WRONG. So we CANNOT pre-load padding as 0 and then apply BN+ReLU. We need to know which pixels are padding and apply BN+ReLU only to real pixels, leaving padding as 0. In my previous approach without shared memory, I had: ```cpp if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { val = inp[...]; val = val * scale + bias; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); } else { val = 0.0f; } ``` This is correct. With shared memory, we can: 1. Load real pixels into xshared (padding already there as 0 from initialization) 2. Apply BN+ReLU in shared memory, but only for real pixels. We need a mask. Simpler: don't pre-apply BN+ReLU to all of shared memory. Instead, in the compute loop, read from xshared and apply the transform with a branch: ```cpp float val = xshared[ih * 18 + iw]; if (ih >= 1 && ih < 17 && iw >= 1 && iw < 17) { // assuming tile not on image boundary val = val * scale + bias; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); } else if (ih < 0 || ih >= H+2 || iw < 0 || iw >= W+2) { val = 0.0f; // padding } else { // real pixel on image boundary val = val * scale + bias; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); } ``` Actually, with the shared memory tile spanning (tile_h-1) to (tile_h+16), and tile_h ranges from 0 to 208 in steps of 16, the tile always covers the entire image area for interior tiles. Boundaries only occur when tile_h=0 (top edge) or tile_h=208 (bottom edge), and similarly for w. For interior tiles (most of them), all 18x18 pixels are valid. Only edge tiles need boundary checks. We can handle this by having the load loop check boundaries, and the compute loop assumes all xshared values are valid (since padding pixels are loaded by the boundary check and set to the correct transformed value, or set to 0). Actually, here's a better approach for shared memory: In the load loop: ```cpp for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 18*18; i += 256) { int local_h = i / 18; int local_w = i % 18; int global_h = tile_h + local_h - 1; int global_w = tile_w + local_w - 1; if (global_h >= 0 && global_h < H && global_w >= 0 && global_w < W) { float v = inp[...]; v = v * scale + bias; v = fmaxf(v, 0.0f); xshared[i] = v; } else { xshared[i] = 0.0f; } } ``` Wait, but `scale` and `bias` are per-channel, and this load loop is inside the ic loop. So we have access to the current channel's scale and bias. This is perfect! We apply BN+ReLU during the load, but ONLY for valid pixels. Invalid pixels get 0.0f. This is correct and efficient! So the kernel becomes: ```cpp template<int IN_CH> __global__ void dense_layer_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ inp, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, int B, int H, int W, int out_C, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE = 16; const int TILE_P = TILE + 2; // 18 int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_idx = blockIdx.y; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; int oc = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; int h = tile_h + tid / TILE; int w = tile_w + tid % TILE; // We use 256 threads, but only first TILE*TILE=256 threads are used for computation // Wait, TILE*TILE = 256. Perfect! __shared__ float wshared[IN_CH * 9]; __shared__ float xshared[TILE_P * TILE_P]; // Load weights for this output channel for (int i = tid; i < IN_CH * 9; i += 256) { wshared[i] = weight[(oc * IN_CH * 9) + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IN_CH; ic++) { float scale = bn_weight[ic]; float bias = bn_bias[ic]; // Load and preprocess input tile for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += 256) { int local_h = i / TILE_P; int local_w = i % TILE_P; int global_h = tile_h + local_h - 1; int global_w = tile_w + local_w - 1; if (global_h >= 0 && global_h < H && global_w >= 0 && global_w < W) { float v = inp[((b * IN_CH + ic) * H + global_h) * W + global_w]; v = v * scale + bias; xshared[i] = fmaxf(v, 0.0f); } else { xshared[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute conv for this thread's output pixel if (h < H && w < W) { int local_h = h - tile_h + 1; int local_w = w - tile_w + 1; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = xshared[(local_h + kh) * TILE_P + (local_w + kw)]; acc += v * wshared[ic * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (h < H && w < W) { int out_ch = out_ch_begin + oc; out[((b * out_C + out_ch) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } } ``` This is a very clean kernel. Let me verify the dimensions: - TILE = 16, so thread count = 16*16 = 256. - tile_idx ranges from 0 to num_tiles-1. - For H=W=224, tiles_h = ceil(224/16) = 14, tiles_w = 14, total tiles = 196. - Grid: (B=10, tiles=196, OUT=32) = (10, 196, 32). Wait, blockIdx.y could be up to 196, but let's verify the calculation: ```cpp int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; // 14 int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; // for tile_idx 0..195: tile_h = 0, 16, ..., 208 int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; // 0, 16, ..., 208 ``` For tile_h=208: h ranges from 208 to 208+15=223. That's valid for H=224. For h=223: local_h = 223-208+1 = 16. local_h+kh ranges from 16 to 18. xshared index ranges from 16*18+16 = 304 to 18*18-1 = 323. That's within 324. Looks correct. Shared memory: - wshared: IN_CH * 9 * 4 bytes. - For IN=32: 1.1KB - For IN=192: 6.9KB - xshared: 18*18*4 = 1.3KB Total: ~8.2KB max. Very small! With 8.2KB shared memory, we can have up to 32 blocks per SM (228/8.2 = 27). With 256 threads per block, 2048/256 = 8 blocks per SM (limited by thread count). So thread count is the limiting factor, giving 100% occupancy (2048 threads). Perfect! Total blocks for layer 5: 10 * 196 * 32 = 62,720. Blocks per SM: 62,720 / 132 = 475. With 8 concurrent blocks per SM, that's 59.4 waves. Great saturation. This kernel should provide excellent performance. Much better than PyTorch's reference because: 1. No concatenation overhead (we write directly to final output) 2. Fused BN+ReLU+Conv into one kernel per layer 3. No temporary allocations for intermediate features 4. Uses shared memory to cache input tiles and weights, reducing global memory traffic 5. 100% occupancy with good parallelism Now, let's think about the overall structure of ModelNew. We need: 1. Store the layer weights (conv weights, BN weights/bias, running_mean, running_var) 2. In forward: - Precompute BN scale and shift for each layer - Allocate output tensor - Copy input - Launch 6 kernels in sequence The kernels need to be exposed via pybind. Since we have 6 different IN_CH values, we can either: a) Write 6 explicitly named functions: `dense_layer_0`, `dense_layer_1`, ... each with the correct template parameter. b) Write one function with a runtime switch. For simplicity and to avoid function pointer overhead, I'll write explicit functions. But with `load_inline`, we can register multiple functions. Actually, we can write a generic launcher that dispatches to the correct template instance. In C++: ```cpp void launch_dense_layer(int in_ch, at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor bn_weight, at::Tensor bn_bias, at::Tensor scale, at::Tensor shift, int out_ch_begin) { int B = inp.size(0); int H = inp.size(2); int W = inp.size(3); int out_C = out.size(1); switch (in_ch) { case 32: launch<32>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, B, H, W, out_C, out_ch_begin); break; case 64: launch<64>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, B, H, W, out_C, out_ch_begin); break; case 96: launch<96>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, B, H, W, out_C, out_ch_begin); break; case 128: launch<128>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, B, H, W, out_C, out_ch_begin); break; case 160: launch<160>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, B, H, W, out_C, out_ch_begin); break; case 192: launch<192>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, B, H, W, out_C, out_ch_begin); break; } } ``` Where `launch` is a function that sets up the grid and launches the kernel. Wait, the input to layer i is `out[:, :in_ch]`. But `out` has total channels = 224. So for layer 0, `inp` should be a slice of `out` with shape [B, 32, H, W]. When launching a CUDA kernel, we can pass the pointer to the start of the slice, and the kernel will use `IN_CH` as the channel stride. Wait, the slice `out[:, :IN_CH]` is contiguous in memory, so `inp.data_ptr<float>()` points to `out.data_ptr<float>() + b*224*H*W + 0*H*W` for batch b. But the kernel is launched with `inp` as the full tensor... actually, we can pass a pointer offset. In PyTorch, `out[:, :IN_CH]` is contiguous, and its `.data_ptr()` is `out.data_ptr() + out.stride(0) * b + out.stride(1) * 0` for the first batch. But we need to pass a pointer that, when accessed with stride IN_CH*H*W between batches, gives the right data. If `out` is contiguous NCHW with 224 channels, its layout is: `out[b, c, h, w]` at `out_ptr + b*224*H*W + c*H*W + h*W + w`. If we pass `out.data_ptr() + b*224*H*W` as the base pointer for batch b, and the kernel uses `IN_CH` as the channel count, then `base + ic*H*W + h*W + w` gives `out[b, ic, h, w]`. This works! But the kernel loops over batch using `blockIdx.x` from 0 to B-1. For a contiguous slice `out[:, :IN_CH]`, the data for batch b starts at `out_ptr + b*224*H*W`. However, since `out[:, :IN_CH]` IS contiguous by itself (it's the leading channels of a contiguous tensor), `.data_ptr()` returns `out_ptr` and `.stride(0)` is `224*H*W`. If we pass `.data_ptr()` directly and use the actual stride between batches (224*H*W), the kernel needs to know this stride, not just IN_CH*H*W. Wait, no. If the kernel assumes contiguous memory with stride `IN_CH*H*W`, but the actual stride is `out_C*H*W` where `out_C=224`, then accessing `base + b*IN_CH*H*W + ic*H*W` would be wrong. So either: 1. We pass the actual batch stride to the kernel, OR 2. We create a temporary contiguous slice `out[:, :IN_CH].contiguous()` and pass that. Option 2 creates a copy! We want to avoid copies. Option 1: Pass the batch stride (which is out_C * H * W). In the kernel: ```cpp int inp_stride_batch = out_C * H * W; int inp_stride_channel = H * W; float val = inp[b * inp_stride_batch + ic * inp_stride_channel + global_h * W + global_w]; ``` Wait, but `out[:, :IN_CH]` IS contiguous in the channel dimension for each batch. The elements are `out[b, 0, 0, 0]`, `out[b, 0, 0, 1]`, ..., `out[b, IN_CH-1, H-1, W-1]`. And then `out[b+1, 0, 0, 0]` is at offset `out_C*H*W`. So the data for batch b starts at `b * out_C * H * W`. So in the kernel, we need two strides: - batch_stride = out_C * H * W - channel_stride = H * W We pass `out.data_ptr()` as `inp`, and set `batch_stride = out_C * H * W`. But for the output, we write to `out[:, out_ch_begin:out_ch_begin+OUT_CH]`. The batch stride is also `out_C * H * W`, and channel stride is `H * W`. So the output write is: ```cpp out[b * out_C * H * W + out_ch * H * W + h * W + w] = acc; ``` Or, we can pass separate `batch_stride` and `channel_stride`. Since `channel_stride` is always `H * W` for contiguous tensors, and `batch_stride` for the output is `out_C * H * W`, we just need `out_C`. In the Python code: ```python out = torch.empty(B, total_C, H, W, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) out[:, :num_input_features] = x ptr = out.data_ptr() batch_stride = total_C * H * W ``` Wait, for layer i, the input is `out[:, :in_ch]`. The elements are at offset `b * batch_stride + c * H * W + h * W + w` for `c` in `[0, in_ch)`. The pointer to start is `out.data_ptr()`. We pass `out.data_ptr()` as `inp_ptr`, and `in_ch` as `IN_CH`, and `batch_stride = total_C * H * W`. For the weight tensor, it should be contiguous. Let me formalize the kernel with strides: ```cpp template<int IN_CH, int TILE=16> __global__ void dense_layer_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ inp, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ scale, const float* __restrict__ shift, int B, int H, int W, int out_C, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE_P = TILE + 2; int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_idx = blockIdx.y; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; int oc = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; int h = tile_h + tid / TILE; int w = tile_w + tid % TILE; __shared__ float wshared[IN_CH * 9]; __shared__ float xshared[TILE_P * TILE_P]; for (int i = tid; i < IN_CH * 9; i += blockDim.x) { wshared[i] = weight[oc * IN_CH * 9 + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc = 0.0f; int batch_offset = b * out_C * H * W; for (int ic = 0; ic < IN_CH; ic++) { float s = scale[ic]; float sh = shift[ic]; for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) { int local_h = i / TILE_P; int local_w = i % TILE_P; int global_h = tile_h + local_h - 1; int global_w = tile_w + local_w - 1; if (global_h >= 0 && global_h < H && global_w >= 0 && global_w < W) { float v = inp[batch_offset + ic * H * W + global_h * W + global_w]; xshared[i] = fmaxf(v * s + sh, 0.0f); } else { xshared[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (h < H && w < W) { int local_h = h - tile_h + 1; int local_w = w - tile_w + 1; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = xshared[(local_h + kh) * TILE_P + (local_w + kw)]; acc += v * wshared[ic * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (h < H && w < W) { out[batch_offset + (out_ch_begin + oc) * H * W + h * W + w] = acc; } } ``` Wait, I notice something. We're using `blockDim.x` which should be 256 (TILE*TILE). But what if H or W is not divisible by TILE? `tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE` handles this. For H=224, W=224, TILE=16, it's exact. For the last tile in each dimension, some threads might have `h >= H` or `w >= W`. We already check `if (h < H && w < W)` before compute and write. But in the shared memory load loop, we need to load the entire 18x18 tile even for edge tiles, because interior threads of the block need the halo. So the load loop runs for all threads (not just those with h<H and w<W). We already do that. One more thing: when tile is on the edge, `global_h` or `global_w` might be out of bounds, and we set `xshared[i] = 0.0f`. This correctly implements zero padding. Now let me think about the kernel launch configuration. For TILE=16, blockDim.x = 256. gridDim: - x = B = 10 - y = ceil(H / 16) * ceil(W / 16) = 14 * 14 = 196 - z = OUT = 32 Actually, I should verify that `(W + TILE - 1) / TILE` is computed correctly. For W=224, TILE=16: (224+15)/16 = 239/16 = 14. Yes. Total blocks: 10 * 196 * 32 = 62,720. That's within CUDA limits (2^31-1 per dimension). Next: in Python, how do we handle the BN parameters? For PyTorch BatchNorm2d in eval mode: `y = (x - running_mean) / sqrt(running_var + eps) * weight + bias` We precompute: `scale = weight / sqrt(running_var + eps)` `shift = bias - running_mean * scale` These are computed on CPU (or GPU) in PyTorch before kernel launch. In ModelNew.__init__(), we need to extract weights from the input layers or directly use the passed layers. Since the task says "Accept the same constructor arguments as Model", the constructor gets num_layers, num_input_features, growth_rate. It doesn't get the actual layers. Wait, how do we get the layer weights? The task says ModelNew should accept the same constructor arguments and implement the same forward. But the reference Model creates random weights. We need ModelNew to produce the same output as a Model with the same constructor arguments. For the evaluation, they probably instantiate `Model` and `ModelNew` with `Model(*get_init_inputs())` and `ModelNew(*get_init_inputs())`. Since `get_init_inputs()` returns [6, 32, 32], both get the same constructor args. But PyTorch initializes weights randomly! So Model and ModelNew would have different weights. Unless there's some seeding. Wait, the evaluation must pass correctness. They either: 1. Use fixed random seeds, or 2. Copy weights from Model to ModelNew, or 3. The evaluation creates one instance and checks against our ModelNew with the same random init. Looking at the task description: "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel. Your ModelNew class must: 1. Accept the same constructor arguments as Model. 2. Implement forward() with the same signature. 3. Produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision." This suggests that they will instantiate Model and ModelNew independently, and expect the same outputs for the same inputs. But ModelNew could create its own layers internally (just like Model) and then extract the weights for the CUDA kernel. That way, if the random seed is the same, both models have the same weights. Actually, PyTorch's `nn.Conv2d` and `nn.BatchNorm2d` use the default initialization, which is deterministic given the same random seed. If the evaluation code sets a seed before creating both models, they'll have the same weights. But what if the evaluation doesn't set a seed? Then we'd need another way. However, standard practice in these tasks is that the evaluator handles this, or we should just create identical layers and extract weights. To be safe, I'll have ModelNew create the same nn.Sequential layers internally (for the same random init), and then in forward(), extract the weights and call the CUDA kernel. Actually, looking at the reference more carefully, we don't necessarily need to use PyTorch to create the layers. We can create the weights manually, but we need to match whatever initialization PyTorch uses. Wait, for correctness, the evaluator will create `Model(*get_init_inputs())` and `ModelNew(*get_init_inputs())`, pass the same input through both, and compare. If the random seeds are controlled, creating the same nn.Module structure will guarantee matching weights. So ModelNew can be: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, num_layers, num_input_features, growth_rate): super().__init__() # Create reference layers to get weights layers = [] for i in range(num_layers): in_ch = num_input_features + i * growth_rate layers.append(nn.Sequential( nn.BatchNorm2d(in_ch), nn.ReLU(inplace=True), nn.Conv2d(in_ch, growth_rate, 3, padding=1, bias=False), nn.Dropout(0.0) )) self.layers = nn.ModuleList(layers) # Register buffers/params - actually ModuleList does this automatically ``` Then in forward, we extract weights from these layers and call the CUDA kernels. Alternatively, we could avoid creating the layers and just register the weights directly as `nn.Parameter` in the correct shapes. But replicating PyTorch's exact initialization is tricky. Better to create the layers as PyTorch modules and let PyTorch initialize them. If ModelNew is constructed after Model in the evaluation code, with the same random state, the weights will match. If they're constructed independently with different random states, they won't match. But that's a problem for ANY solution. Actually, the typical evaluation pattern is: ```python model = Model(*get_init_inputs()) model_new = ModelNew(*get_init_inputs()) # Check that model_new produces same output as model ``` For this to work, they must either: 1. Set the same seed before each, or 2. Copy weights from model to model_new. I suspect they might copy weights, or they might create model_new and then somehow ensure weights match. This means for a given model state (weights), the forward must match. And also provide a `_load_from_ref` if needed, but that's not required by the interface. Let me just create the same nn.ModuleList structure. Actually, for performance in forward, we want to precompute and store the BN scales/shifts, and store contiguous weight tensors. In `__init__`: - Create the layers - After creation, extract all parameters - During first forward or in init, compute `scale` and `shift` for each BN - Also, transpose/reshape conv weights for optimal memory access if needed For the conv weights in PyTorch: shape is [out_ch, in_ch, 3, 3]. In the kernel, we access `weight[oc * IN_CH * 9 + local_idx]`. This assumes weights are stored as [oc, ic, kh, kw] contiguous. PyTorch's default is [oc, ic, kh, kw] in NCHW-like order for conv weights. Actually, PyTorch conv weight shape is (out_channels, in_channels, kernel_h, kernel_w) and is contiguous in that order. So `weight[oc, ic, kh, kw]` corresponds to `weight[((oc * IN_CH) + ic) * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]`. Yes, this matches our kernel! For BatchNorm2d, the parameters are: - `running_mean`: shape [in_ch] - `running_var`: shape [in_ch] - `weight` (gamma): shape [in_ch] - `bias` (beta): shape [in_ch] We compute scale and shift at runtime or precompute them. Now, there's a subtle issue with `nn.ReLU(inplace=True)`. In the reference model, ReLU modifies its input in place. For our kernel, we don't need to worry about this because we fuse it. Let me write out the complete ModelNew code. Actually, before writing the full kernel, I want to consider one more optimization: using `__restrict__` and ensuring memory writes don't conflict. PyTorch tensors in NCHW format on CUDA: - batch stride = C * H * W - channel stride = H * W - height stride = W - width stride = 1 For `out[:, :IN_CH]`, even though it's a slice of a larger tensor, the data is contiguous within the slice (the channels 0..IN_CH-1 are contiguous in memory for each batch). Here's the plan for the Python module: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' template<int IN_CH, int TILE=16> __global__ void dense_layer_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ inp, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ scale, const float* __restrict__ shift, int B, int H, int W, int out_C, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE_P = TILE + 2; break; case 160: launch_dense_layer<160>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, out_ch_begin); break; case 192: launch_dense_layer<192>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, out_ch_begin); break; } } ''' cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> void dense_layer_forward(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight, at::Tensor scale, at::Tensor shift, int in_ch, int out_ch_begin); Now, for the Python ModelNew class: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, num_layers, num_input_features, growth_rate): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.num_layers = num_layers self.num_input_features = num_input_features self.growth_rate = growth_rate layers = [] for i in range(num_layers): in_ch = num_input_features + i * growth_rate layers.append(nn.Sequential( nn.BatchNorm2d(in_ch), nn.ReLU(inplace=True), nn.Conv2d(in_ch, growth_rate, kernel_size=3, padding=1, bias=False), nn.Dropout(0.0) )) self.layers = nn.ModuleList(layers) # Pre-extract and cache BN params and conv weights for fast access self._scales = [] self._shifts = [] self._weights = [] for layer in self.layers: bn = layer[0] conv = layer[2] # Precompute scale and shift # BN: y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma + beta eps = bn.eps scale = bn.weight / torch.sqrt(bn.running_var + eps) shift = bn.bias - bn.running_mean * scale self._scales.append(scale.detach().contiguous()) self._shifts.append(shift.detach().contiguous()) self._weights.append(conv.weight.detach().contiguous().view(conv.weight.size(0), -1)) # Wait, conv.weight shape is [out_ch, in_ch, 3, 3] # For the kernel, we need [out_ch, in_ch*9]. Also, I realize there's another logical bug in my first kernel. The reference computes `new_feature = layer(x)` where `x` is the concatenation of all features so far. But there's a problem: `out[:, :in_ch]` is a non-contiguous view (strided) if `out` has more channels than `in_ch`. Actually, `out[:, :in_ch]` on a 4D tensor `(B, C, H, W)` is contiguous if `in_ch == C`, but if `in_ch < C`, then the stride along the channel dimension is still `H*W`, and the elements are contiguous in memory because there are no gaps between channels within the slice. Actually it does: `H*W`, and the next stride is `W`, then `1`. The memory is perfectly packed. But the stride between batch elements is `C*H*W` instead of `in_ch*H*W`. PyTorch's `is_contiguous()` checks if the strides match the standard C-contiguous layout. For a 4D tensor `(B, C, H, W)`, standard stride is `(C*H*W, H*W, W, 1)`. Here `out[:, :in_ch]` has stride `(C*H*W, H*W, W, 1)`. Since `C` in the stride is `out_C`, and `out_C` != `in_ch` (unless it's the last step), this tensor is NOT contiguous in the sense of PyTorch. If I pass a non-contiguous tensor to my CUDA kernel, I need to either copy it to a contiguous buffer, or handle the striding in the kernel. The simplest approach is to pass the full `out` tensor as input and indicate the number of input channels, then the kernel reads from it using `out_C * H * W` as batch stride. The input to layer `i` is `x` (a contiguous tensor). In my `ModelNew`, I create `out` as a contiguous tensor `(B, total_C, H, W)`. I set `out[:, :num_input_features] = x`. Then for layer 0, `out[:, :32]` is NOT contiguous in PyTorch's definition (batch stride is total_C*H*W = 224*224*224, but `in_ch` stride is 32*224*224). Wait, PyTorch considers a tensor contiguous if the strides match exactly what you'd get from `tensor.contiguous()`. For a 4D tensor `(B, C, H, W)`, contiguous means stride `(C*H*W, H*W, W, 1)`. For `out[:, :32]`, the stride is `(224*224*224, 224*224, 224, 1)`. This is NOT what you'd get for a `(10, 32, 224, 224)` contiguous tensor, which would have stride `(32*224*224, 224*224, 224, 1)`. So `out[:, :32].is_contiguous()` returns `False`. However, in terms of memory layout, all channels are packed together in memory for each batch element. Let me check how `load_inline` works internally... In PyTorch, `load_inline` generates a C++ file that includes: 1. `#include <torch/extension.h>` 2. `#include <pybind11/pybind11.h>` 3. The content of `cpp_sources`. 4. Declarations for the functions listed in `functions` (it might actually just call `m.def(...)` for each function name, assuming they are defined somewhere visible). Actually, looking at PyTorch source, `load_inline` does the following: - It creates a `.cpp` file containing `cpp_sources` and a `PYBIND11_MODULE` block that binds functions in `functions`. - It creates a `.cu` file if `cuda_sources` is provided. - It compiles them together. i < IN_CH * 9; i += blockDim.x) {\n wshared[i] = weight[oc * IN_CH * 9 + i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n float acc = 0.0f;\n int inp_batch_stride = out_C * H * W;\n\n for (int ic = 0; ic < IN_CH; ic++) {\n float s = scale[ic];\n float sh = shift[ic];\n\n for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) {\n int local_h = i / TILE_P;\n int local_w = i % TILE_P;\n int global_h = tile_h + local_h - 1;\n int global_w = tile_w + local_w - 1;\n if (global_h >= 0 && global_h < H && global_w >= 0 && global_w < W) {\n float v = inp[inp_batch_stride * b + ic * H * W + global_h * W + global_w];\n xshared[i] = fmaxf(v * s + sh, 0.0f);\n } else {\n xshared[i] = 0.0f;\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n if (h < H && w < W) {\n int local_h = h - tile_h + 1;\n int local_w = w - tile_w + 1;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) {\n float v = xshared[(local_h + kh) * TILE_P + (local_w + kw)];\n acc += v * wshared[ic * 9 + kh * 3 + kw];\n }\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n }\n\n if (h < H && w < W) {\n out[inp_batch_stride * b + (out_ch_begin + oc) * H * W + h * W + w] = acc;\n }\n}\n\ntemplate<int IN_CH>\nvoid launch_dense_layer(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight,\n at::Tensor scale, at::Tensor shift, int out_ch_begin) {\n int B = inp.size(0);\n int H = inp.size(2);\n int W = inp.size(3);\n int out_C = out.size(1);\n\n const int TILE = 16;\n int tiles_h = (H + TILE - 1) / TILE;\n int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE;\n int num_tiles = tiles_h * tiles_w;\n\n dim3 grid(B, num_tiles, 32);\n dim3 block(TILE * TILE);\n\n dense_layer_kernel<IN_CH><<<grid, block>>>(\n out.data_ptr<float>(),\n inp.data_ptr<float>(),\n weight.data_ptr<float>(),\n scale.data_ptr<float>(),\n shift.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, H, W, out_C, out_ch_begin\n );\n}\n\nvoid dense_layer_forward(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight,\n at::Tensor scale, at::Tensor shift, int in_ch, int out_ch_begin) {\n switch (in_ch) {\n case 32: launch_dense_layer<32>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, out_ch_begin); break;\n case 64: launch_dense_layer<64>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, out_ch_begin); break;\n case 96: launch_dense_layer<96>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, out_ch_begin); break;\n case 128: launch_dense_layer<128>(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, out_ch_begin); Actually, looking at my original code again, I think there may have been a serious issue: I was making `out` the same tensor for input and output, and in the kernel reading from `inp` (which pointed to `out.data_ptr()`) while writing to `out`. Even though the regions don't overlap, the compiler might optimize this in weird ways. Using `__restrict__` on both should tell the compiler they don't alias, but since they're actually the SAME pointer, this is a LIE! The `__restrict__` qualifier promises that the pointer doesn't alias any other pointer, but here `inp` and `out` are the SAME pointer! This is undefined behavior! YES! That's the bug! In my first attempt: ```cpp void dense_layer_forward(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, ...) ``` And in Python: ```python mod.dense_layer_forward(out, out[:, :in_ch], ...) ``` But `out[:, :in_ch].data_ptr()` == `out.data_ptr()` (same memory). And in the kernel signature: ```cpp float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ inp, ``` These are passed as DIFFERENT arguments but they point to the SAME memory. The `__restrict__` keyword tells the compiler that these pointers don't alias. But they DO! This is a violation of the restrict contract, and the compiler is free to reorder reads and writes in ways that break correctness! THAT'S IT! This is why the numerical results were wrong! The compiler, seeing `__restrict__`, might have reordered or cached reads, not realizing that writes to `out` affect reads from `inp`. In my second attempt, I did `out[:, :in_ch].contiguous()`, which created a separate tensor with a DIFFERENT data_ptr. So `inp` and `out` were different pointers. But then I had the batch stride bug. So the fix is: either don't use `__restrict__` when `inp` and `out` might alias, or ensure they don't alias. Or better yet, just remove `__restrict__` from both, or pass them correctly. Actually, even without `__restrict__`, reading and writing to the same memory from the same kernel can be problematic if not careful, but on GPUs within a single thread, reads and writes to global memory are generally ordered. The issue with `__restrict__` specifically is that the compiler might optimize based on the assumption that they don't alias, which can cause incorrect code generation. For safety, I'll make sure `inp` and `out` are different tensors. So I'll use `x = out[:, :in_ch].contiguous()` as input, and write to `out` as output. And I'll fix the batch stride computation. ''' cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void dense_layer_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ inp, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ scale, const float* __restrict__ shift, int B, int H, int W, int in_ch, int out_C, int growth_rate, int out_ch_begin ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; } ''' mod = load_inline(...) ``` Then in forward: ```python def forward(self, x): B, C, H, W = x.shape out = torch.empty(B, self.total_C, H, W, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) out[:, :self.num_input_features] = x current_end = self.num_input_features for i in range(self.num_layers): scale = getattr(self, f'scale_{i}') shift = getattr(self, f'shift_{i}') weight = getattr(self, f'weight_{i}') inp = out[:, :current_end].contiguous() mod.dense_layer_forward(out, inp, weight, scale, shift, current_end) current_end += self.growth_rate return out ``` I think this should work! But in `xshared` loading: ```c for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) { int local_h = i / TILE_P; // i / 18 int local_w = i % TILE_P; // i % 18 ``` Wait, TILE_P = TILE + 2 = 18. So local_h goes from 0 to 17, local_w from 0 to 17. Then: ```c int global_h = tile_h + local_h - 1; // tile_h + [0..17] - 1 = tile_h - 1 to tile_h + 16 int global_w = tile_w + local_w - 1; // similar ``` For tile_h = 0, global_h ranges from -1 to 16. For tile_h = 208 (last tile if H=224 and TILE=16: tiles at 0, 16, ..., 208), global_h ranges from 207 to 224. But H=224, so valid h is 0..223. global_h=224 is out of bounds, which should be caught by the if statement. Good. Then when reading from `xshared`: ```c int local_h = h - tile_h + 1; // h in [tile_h, tile_h+15], so local_h in [1, 16] int local_w = w - tile_w + 1; // similar #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = xshared[(local_h + kh) * TILE_P + (local_w + kw)]; ``` For local_h = 16 and kh = 2, index = (16+2)*18 + ... = 18*18 = 324. But xshared has TILE_P * TILE_P = 18*18 = 324 elements, so valid indices are 0..323. 324 is out of bounds! THIS IS THE BUG! When h = tile_h + 15 = tile_h + TILE - 1, local_h = TILE = 16. Then local_h + kh can be 16 + 2 = 18 = TILE_P. The index becomes TILE_P * TILE_P + ... which is out of bounds! The xshared array has indices [0, TILE_P-1] for both dimensions. When local_h = TILE, we need to access xshared[TILE + 2][...] which is xshared[18][...] = index 18*18 + ... The index is (local_h + kh) * TILE_P + (local_w + kw) = 18 * 18 + ... which is out of bounds (max valid is 17*18+17=323, 18*18=324). To fix this, the shared memory tile needs to cover [tile_h-1, tile_h+TILE], which is TILE+2 elements. For h in [tile_h, tile_h+TILE-1], when kh=2, the max row needed is h+2 = tile_h+TILE+1.
