Let me write the kernel: ```cuda template<int TILE=16> __global__ void dense_layer_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ inp, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ scale, const float* __restrict__ shift, int B, int H, int W, int in_ch, int out_stride, int in_stride, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE_P = TILE + 3; int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_idx = blockIdx.y; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; int oc = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; int local_h = tid / TILE; int local_w = tid % TILE; int h = tile_h + local_h; int w = tile_w + local_w; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* wshared = smem; float* xshared = smem + in_ch * 9; for (int i = tid; i < in_ch * 9; i += blockDim.x) { wshared[i] = weight[oc * in_ch * 9 + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_ch; ic++) { float s = scale[ic]; float sh = shift[ic]; for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) { int lh = i / TILE_P; int lw = i % TILE_P; int gh = tile_h + lh - 1; int gw = tile_w + lw - 1; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { float v = inp[b * in_stride + ic * H * W + gh * W + gw]; xshared[i] = fmaxf(v * s + sh, 0.0f); } else { xshared[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (h < H && w < W) { int lh2 = local_h + 1; int lw2 = local_w + 1; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = xshared[(lh2 + kh) * TILE_P + (lw2 + kw)]; acc += v * wshared[ic * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (h < H && w < W) { out[b * out_stride + (out_ch_begin + oc) * H * W + h * W + w] = acc; } } ``` Wait, `local_h` and `local_w` are computed from `tid / TILE` and `tid % TILE`. I'll use PyTorch for BN (train mode correctness), and my kernel for ReLU+Conv. Here's the final code structure: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # C++ header cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> void relu_conv_forward(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight, int out_ch_begin); ''' # CUDA kernel cuda_source = ''' #include <cuda_runtime.h> template<int TILE=16> __global__ void relu_conv_kernel(...) { // shared memory for weight and input tile // load weight // for each input channel: // load tile with halo, apply ReLU // accumulate conv result // write output } void relu_conv_forward(...) { // launch kernel } ''' mod = load_inline(...) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, num_layers, num_input_features, growth_rate): super().__init__() self.num_layers = num_layers self.num_input_features = num_input_features self.growth_rate = growth_rate # Create original layers to preserve BN behavior layers = [] for i in range(num_layers): in_ch = num_input_features + i * growth_rate layers.append(nn.Sequential( nn.BatchNorm2d(in_ch), nn.ReLU(inplace=True), nn.Conv2d(in_ch, growth_rate, kernel_size=3, padding=1, bias=False), nn.Dropout(0.0) )) self.layers = nn.ModuleList(layers) # Pre-extract conv weights for the custom kernel for i, layer in enumerate(self.layers): conv = layer[2] self.register_buffer(f"weight_{i}", conv.weight.view(conv.out_channels, -1).contiguous()) def forward(self, x): B, C, H, W = x.shape total_C = self.num_input_features + self.num_layers * self.growth_rate out = torch.empty(B, total_C, H, W, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) out[:, :self.num_input_features] = x current_end = self.num_input_features for i, layer in enumerate(self.layers): bn = layer[0] conv = layer[2] inp = out[:, :current_end] # Exact BN (handles train/eval mode correctly) normalized = bn(inp) # Apply ReLU (inplace not possible since normalized might be needed, but it's a new tensor) activated = torch.relu(normalized) # Custom kernel for Conv weight = self.get_buffer(f"weight_{i}") mod.relu_conv_forward(out, activated, weight, current_end) current_end += self.growth_rate return out ``` Wait, the `nn.ReLU(inplace=True)` in the original layers might still cause issues if we call the layer. But we're not calling the whole layer, just `bn()` and then doing ReLU separately. Actually, `torch.relu(normalized)` creates a new tensor with ReLU applied. That's fine. For the kernel, it reads `inp` (which is `activated`) and writes to `out`. These don't overlap. Let me write the actual kernel code now. I'll base it on the corrected tile approach: ```cuda template<int TILE=16> __global__ void relu_conv_kernel( float* out, const float* inp, const float* weight, int B, int H, int W, int in_ch, int out_stride, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE_P = TILE + 3; int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_idx = blockIdx.y; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; int oc = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; int local_h = tid / TILE; int local_w = tid % TILE; int h = tile_h + local_h; int w = tile_w + local_w; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* wshared = smem; float* xshared = smem + in_ch * 9; for (int i = tid; i < in_ch * 9; i += blockDim.x) { wshared[i] = weight[oc * in_ch * 9 + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_ch; ic++) { for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) { int lh = i / TILE_P; int lw = i % TILE_P; int gh = tile_h + lh - 1; int gw = tile_w + lw - 1; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { float v = inp[b * in_ch * H * W + ic * H * W + gh * W + gw]; xshared[i] = fmaxf(v, 0.0f); // ReLU (already applied in Python, but do it again just in case? Or remove?) } else { xshared[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (h < H && w < W) { int lh2 = local_h + 1; int lw2 = local_w + 1; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = xshared[(lh2 + kh) * TILE_P + (lw2 + kw)]; acc += v * wshared[ic * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } __syncthreads(); } if (h < H && w < W) { out[b * out_stride + (out_ch_begin + oc) * H * W + h * W + w] = acc; } } ``` Wait, the activated input already has ReLU applied. But applying ReLU again doesn't hurt (it's idempotent for non-negative values). Actually, `fmaxf(v, 0)` on already-ReLU'd data is a no-op, so it's fine but unnecessary. Since ReLU was already applied in Python, I can just read `v` directly without applying ReLU again: ```c xshared[i] = v; // No ReLU needed ``` This avoids the redundant operation. Let me write the kernel without the redundant ReLU. Also, I need to be careful about `in_stride`. For `activated`, which is the output of `torch.relu(bn(inp))`, it has shape (B, in_ch, H, W) and is contiguous. So `in_stride = in_ch * H * W`. In the kernel: ```c float v = inp[b * in_ch * H * W + ic * H * W + gh * W + gw]; ``` This assumes `inp` is contiguous. Since `activated` is produced by `torch.relu(normalized)`, it's contiguous. Good. For `out`, `out_stride = out.stride(0) = total_C * H * W`. I should pass `in_stride` as a parameter to handle both contiguous and non-contiguous, but since `activated` is contiguous, I can just use `in_ch * H * W` directly. Or pass `inp.stride(0)` to be safe. Actually, I'll pass the strides explicitly. One more thing: the `xshared` size. With `TILE=16` and `TILE_P=19`, each block needs `in_ch * 9 + 361` floats of shared memory. For `in_ch=192`, that's `1728 + 361 = 2089` floats = 8356 bytes. This is well within limits. For the grid: `dim3 grid(B, num_tiles, growth_rate)` where `growth_rate=32` and `num_tiles=(224/16)^2=196`. So `grid = (10, 196, 32)`. Total blocks = `10 * 196 * 32 = 62720`. For the H100 with 132 SMs (I think H100 has 132 or 144 SMs depending on SKU), launching 62720 blocks gives about 475 blocks per SM. With each block taking ~256 threads and having high compute intensity, this should keep the GPU busy. Actually wait, I need to be careful about H100 specs. I was given that the SMs can run 32 thread blocks each. With 62720 blocks and 132 SMs, that's about 475 blocks per SM, which should saturate the GPU. Now, one concern: the kernel does a lot of shared memory operations and compute. Each `ic` iteration loads a 19x19 tile into shared memory (361 floats = ~1.4 KB for 32 channels, up to ~8.4 KB for 192 channels) and reads a 3x3 neighborhood for each output pixel. With 32 output channels (blockIdx.z), each block reads the same input tile but different weights. This means input tiles are loaded 32 times (once per output channel) but weights are different. Actually, there might be a better way to organize the kernel: have each block compute a tile for ALL output channels, not just one. This way, the input tile is loaded once and reused for all 32 output channels. Let me reorganize: - blockIdx.x = b - blockIdx.y = tile_idx - blockDim.x = 256 threads (16x16 tile) - Each thread computes 16x16 output pixels for one output channel, but we serialize over output channels. - OR, each block computes a 16x16 tile for all 32 output channels, with threads cooperating. Actually, let me keep the current design but change it so each block computes all 32 output channels: - blockIdx.x = b - blockIdx.y = tile_idx - blockDim.x = 256 = 16x16 threads, each handling one spatial pixel - Each thread computes ALL 32 output channels for its pixel. This reduces the number of blocks from 62720 to 1960 (since we don't have the z-dimension), which might lead to better occupancy. But each thread now does 32x more work (computing 32 output channels instead of 1). With 256 threads per block, total threads = 1960 * 256 = 501,760. Each thread computes 32 output channels * ~192*9 FMAs = ~55K FMAs. That's a LOT of work per thread but fewer thread blocks. Actually, for an H100 with many SMs, having more thread blocks is generally better for hiding latency. But 1960 blocks should be enough for 132 SMs (about 15 blocks per SM). Each block has high compute intensity, so this might be fine. But 55K FMAs per thread means each thread runs for a long time, which might cause load imbalance if some threads finish earlier. Let me stick with the original design where blockIdx.z is the output channel, giving more blocks and finer parallelism. Actually, I just realized: with blockIdx.z = 32 and blockDim.x = 256, each block has 256 threads. For a 16x16 tile, exactly 256 pixels. So each thread handles exactly one pixel. For some boundary tiles, not all 256 pixels are valid. But since 224 is divisible by 16, all tiles are fully valid, so all 256 threads are active. Good. For in_ch = 192, each thread does 192 * 9 = 1728 FMAs. At ~2 FLOPs per FMA, that's ~3456 FLOPs per thread. With 62720 * 256 = 16,056,320 threads, total FLOPs = ~55.5 GFLOPs for one layer. At 67 TFLOPS, this is ~1.4ms of compute (theoretical peak). With memory overhead, maybe 2-3ms. The reference with repeated cat has much higher memory overhead. Let me write the complete final kernel code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> void relu_conv_forward(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight, int out_ch_begin); ''' cuda_source = r''' #include <cuda_runtime.h> template<int TILE=16> __global__ void relu_conv_kernel( float* out, const float* inp, const float* weight, int B, int H, int W, int in_ch, int out_stride, int in_stride, int growth_rate, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE_P = TILE + 3; int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_idx = blockIdx.y; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; int oc = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; i < in_ch * 9; i += blockDim.x) { wshared[i] = weight[oc * in_ch * 9 + i]; } __syncthreads(); float acc = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_ch; ic++) { for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) { int lh = i / TILE_P; relu_conv_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); } ''' mod = load_inline( name="relu_conv_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["relu_conv_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, num_layers: int, num_input_features: int, growth_rate: int): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.num_layers = num_layers self.num_input_features = num_input_features self.growth_rate = growth_rate layers = [] for i in range(num_layers): in_ch = num_input_features + i * growth_rate layers.append(nn.Sequential( nn.BatchNorm2d(in_ch), nn.ReLU(inplace=True), nn.Conv2d(in_ch, growth_rate, kernel_size=3, padding=1, bias=False), nn.Dropout(0.0) )) self.layers = nn.ModuleList(layers) for i, layer in enumerate(self.layers): conv = layer[2] self.register_buffer(f"weight_{i}", conv.weight.view(conv.out_channels, -1).contiguous()) def forward(self, x): B, C, H, W = x.shape total_C = self.num_input_features + self.num_layers * self.growth_rate out = torch.empty(B, total_C, H, W, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) out[:, :self.num_input_features] = x current_end = self.num_input_features for i, layer in enumerate(self.layers): bn = layer[0] conv = layer[2] inp = out[:, :current_end] normalized = bn(inp) activated = torch.relu(normalized) weight = self.get_buffer(f"weight_{i}") mod.relu_conv_forward(out, activated, weight, current_end) current_end += self.growth_rate return out ``` Wait, I apply `torch.relu` after `bn`, but `bn` output might already include the ReLU if I used the full layer. Actually, a safer approach is to just not use an array: ```c float acc0 = 0, acc1 = 0, acc2 = 0, acc3 = 0, acc4 = 0, acc5 = 0, acc6 = 0, acc7 = 0; ... acc0 += v * w0; acc1 += v * w1; ... ``` But this is tedious. Let me try the array approach with `#pragma unroll` first. Also, for the weight loading, I need to load weight[oc_base + oc][ic][kh][kw] for oc in [0, 8). Since weight is 1D with shape (32, in_ch*9), the indexing is: `weight[(oc_base + oc) * in_ch * 9 + ic * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]` Let me finalize the kernel: ```c template<int TILE=16, int OC_PER_BLOCK=8> __global__ void fused_conv_kernel(...) { const int TILE_P = TILE + 3; int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_idx = blockIdx.y; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; int oc_base = blockIdx.z * OC_PER_BLOCK; int tid = threadIdx.x; int local_h = tid / TILE; int local_w = tid % TILE; int h = tile_h + local_h; int w = tile_w + local_w; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* xshared = smem; float* wshared = smem + TILE_P * TILE_P; float acc[OC_PER_BLOCK]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_PER_BLOCK; i++) { acc[i] = 0.0f; } for (int ic = 0; ic < in_ch; ic++) { // load input tile for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) { int lh = i / TILE_P; int lw = i % TILE_P; int gh = tile_h + lh - 1; int gw = tile_w + lw - 1; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { xshared[i] = inp[b * in_stride + ic * H * W + gh * W + gw]; } else { xshared[i] = 0.0f; } } // load weights for this ic and all oc in block for (int i = tid; i < OC_PER_BLOCK * 9; i += blockDim.x) { int oc = i / 9; int offset = i % 9; wshared[i] = weight[(oc_base + oc) * in_ch * 9 + ic * 9 + offset]; } __syncthreads(); if (h < H && w < W) { int lh2 = local_h + 1; int lw2 = local_w + 1; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = xshared[(lh2 + kh) * TILE_P + (lw2 + kw)]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < OC_PER_BLOCK; oc++) { acc[oc] += v * wshared[oc * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; } } } } __syncthreads(); } if (h < H && w < W) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < OC_PER_BLOCK; oc++) { out[b * out_stride + (out_ch_begin + oc_base + oc) * H * W + h * W + w] = acc[oc]; } } } ``` This design loads each input tile once per block and each weight set once per ic. Much more efficient! Wait, but `xshared` is loaded once per `ic` iteration, which is correct. For each `ic`, we load the spatial tile (361 floats) once, and all 8 output channels reuse it. Total shared memory loads per block = in_ch * 361 = 192 * 361 = ~69K floats. For 7840 blocks, that's 541M floats = ~2.1GB. This is 8x less than the redundant design (since we compute 8 oc per block instead of 1). Good. But I should verify that this actually works. I need to pass `growth_rate=32` and compute `num_oc_groups = growth_rate / OC_PER_BLOCK = 4`. Shared memory per block: TILE_P*TILE_P + OC_PER_BLOCK*9 = 361 + 72 = 433 floats = ~1.7KB. Very comfortable. For growth_rate=32, oc_group goes from 0 to 3. If growth_rate isn't divisible by OC_PER_BLOCK, we'd need to handle the remainder. But 32/8=4, so it's fine. Actually, I should make OC_PER_BLOCK flexible or just hardcode for this problem. Since growth_rate is a constructor parameter but for this benchmark it's fixed at 32, I can assume it's divisible by OC_PER_BLOCK. But in the __init__, I should check or handle it. For simplicity, I'll just use a template with OC_PER_BLOCK=8. Hmm, but what if the kernel is called with growth_rate not divisible by 8? Let me add a check in the C++ wrapper. Actually, I can just use `blockIdx.z` from 0 to (growth_rate + OC_PER_BLOCK - 1) / OC_PER_BLOCK - 1, and handle the last block specially if growth_rate isn't divisible by OC_PER_BLOCK. But for simplicity and since the test uses growth_rate=32, I'll just require divisibility or use a dynamic loop with `oc < min(OC_PER_BLOCK, growth_rate - oc_base)`. Let me use the dynamic upper bound for safety: ```c int oc_limit = min(OC_PER_BLOCK, growth_rate - oc_base); ``` And in loops: ```c for (int oc = 0; oc < oc_limit; oc++) { ... } ``` But `#pragma unroll` doesn't work with dynamic bounds. For the correctness, I can unroll with a fixed OC_PER_BLOCK and just only write the valid outputs. Or I can avoid unrolling. Given the time, let me just write the kernel with a fixed OC_PER_BLOCK=8 and assume growth_rate is divisible by 8, which it is (32). Wait, actually I should be extra cautious. Let me just make the kernel work for any growth_rate by using a dynamic loop. The compiler might still optimize it well since the value is a runtime constant passed to the kernel. Hmm, the performance difference between unrolled and non-unrolled inner loop might be significant. Let me use `#pragma unroll` but pad the acc array to OC_PER_BLOCK: ```c #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < OC_PER_BLOCK; oc++) { if (oc_base + oc < growth_rate) { acc[oc] += ...; } } ``` With `#pragma unroll`, the compiler generates 8 checks, but 4 of them (for oc_base = 24, growth_rate=32) would be true, and if oc_base=32, none would be true (but this block wouldn't exist). So the check is technically unnecessary if I ensure the grid only launches valid blocks. Let me ensure the grid is sized correctly: ```python num_oc_groups = (growth_rate + OC_PER_BLOCK - 1) // OC_PER_BLOCK ``` For growth_rate=32, num_oc_groups=4. Each group handles 8 output channels. The last group handles channels 24-31, which is exactly 8 channels. All good. OK, I think this is a solid design. Let me write the complete code. Actually, I want to double check one thing: the xshared loading. For each `ic`, I have: ```c for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) { int lh = i / TILE_P; int lw = i % TILE_P; int gh = tile_h + lh - 1; int gw = tile_w + lw - 1; ``` With TILE_P = 19, lh ranges 0..18, lw ranges 0..18. gh = tile_h - 1 + lh. For tile_h=0, gh ranges -1 to 17. For tile_h=208, gh ranges 207 to 225. But H=224, so valid gh is 0..223. The if statement `if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W)` handles out-of-bounds. Then when accessing: ```c int lh2 = local_h + 1; // local_h in [0, 15], so lh2 in [1, 16] int lw2 = local_w + 1; // lw2 in [1, 16] xshared[(lh2 + kh) * TILE_P + (lw2 + kw)] ``` For local_h=15, kh=2: index = (15+2)*19 + ... = 17*19 + ... = 323 + ... Max index: 17*19 + 17 = 340. TILE_P*TILE_P = 19*19 = 361. So 340 < 361. Valid! And the corresponding global row: tile_h + (lh2 + kh) - 1 = tile_h + local_h + kh = h + kh. Since h = tile_h + local_h. For h=223 (max valid), kh=2: gh=225, which is out of bounds. And xshared is loaded with gh=225 being assigned 0. So xshared[17][...] = 0. Correct. Great, this should work. Let me now compile, test correctness, and submit if correct. I have enough tool calls. Wait, actually I'm second-guessing myself. Let me think about if using `#pragma unroll` with `acc[oc]` will actually use registers. If the compiler decides to put `acc` in local memory, performance could be terrible. Local memory access has the same latency as global memory. But `float acc[8]` with `pragma unroll` and a constant access pattern SHOULD be kept in registers. The NVIDIA compiler is generally good about this for small fixed-size arrays. Actually, one more thing: `wshared[oc * 9 + ...]` - the `oc` loop is inside the `kh, kw` loops. For each `v` read from xshared, we multiply it by 8 different weights. This is exactly what the compiler should optimize well. For each `(kh, kw)` pair: 1. Read `v` from shared memory once. 2. Use it in 8 FMAs with 8 different weights. 3. The 8 weights are read from `wshared` which is also in shared memory. Since there are 9 `(kh, kw)` pairs, we do 9 reads from xshared and 72 reads from wshared per output pixel per `ic`. Wait, but `v` is the same for all 8 output channels! So we read `v` once and reuse it 8 times. And we read 8 weights from wshared. Total shared memory reads = 1 (for v) + 8 (for weights) = 9 reads per output pixel per `ic`. That's very efficient! Compare to doing 8 separate convolutions: we'd read `v` 8 times. So this design is 8x more efficient in shared memory reads for the input tile. And we write 8 outputs at the end, but those are to global memory. This is actually a great kernel design! OK, now I need to actually write the full code properly. Let me be very careful with indexing and ensure correctness. For the weight tensor passed from Python: `self.register_buffer(f"weight_{i}", conv.weight.view(conv.out_channels, -1).contiguous())` This has shape (growth_rate, in_ch * 9). In the kernel, I access it as: `weight[(oc_base + oc) * in_ch * 9 + ic * 9 + offset]` where `offset = kh * 3 + kw`. This matches the view layout. One concern: the C++ wrapper function receives `at::Tensor weight`. I need to make sure it's contiguous and on the right device. Since I registered it as a buffer with `.contiguous()`, it should be fine. Alright, I'll write the complete code now and test. Actually, I want to be very sure about the Python-C++ binding. The function signature in C++ should match what PyTorch generates: ```c++ void relu_conv_forward(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight, int out_ch_begin); ``` And in Python: ```python mod.relu_conv_forward(out, activated, weight, current_end) ``` The arguments are passed as C++ Tensors and an int. Should be fine. One more thing: error checking. I should probably add `TORCH_CHECK` to validate tensor dimensions and device. But the kernel will likely fail with an error if something is wrong. Let me write the full code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> void relu_conv_forward(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight, int out_ch_begin); ''' cuda_source = r''' #include <cuda_runtime.h> template<int TILE=16, int OC_PER_BLOCK=8> __global__ void relu_conv_kernel( float* out, const float* inp, const float* weight, int B, int H, int W, int in_ch, int out_stride, int in_stride, int growth_rate, int out_ch_begin ) { const int TILE_P = TILE + 3; int b = blockIdx.x; int tile_idx = blockIdx.y; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tile_h = (tile_idx / tiles_w) * TILE; int tile_w = (tile_idx % tiles_w) * TILE; int oc_base = blockIdx.z * OC_PER_BLOCK; float acc[OC_PER_BLOCK]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < OC_PER_BLOCK; i++) { acc[i] = 0.0f; } for (int ic = 0; ic < in_ch; ic++) { for (int i = tid; i < TILE_P * TILE_P; i += blockDim.x) { int lh = i / TILE_P; int lw = i % TILE_P; int gh = tile_h + lh - 1; int gw = tile_w + lw - 1; if (gh >= 0 && gh < H && gw >= 0 && gw < W) { xshared[i] = inp[b * in_stride + ic * H * W + gh * W + gw]; } else { xshared[i] = 0.0f; } } for (int i = tid; i < OC_PER_BLOCK * 9; i += blockDim.x) { wshared[i] = weight[(oc_base + i / 9) * in_ch * 9 + ic * 9 + (i % 9)]; } __syncthreads(); if (h < H && w < W) { int lh2 = local_h + 1; int lw2 = local_w + 1; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { float v = xshared[(lh2 + kh) * TILE_P + (lw2 + kw)]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < OC_PER_BLOCK; } } } } void relu_conv_forward(at::Tensor out, at::Tensor inp, at::Tensor weight, int out_ch_begin) { int B = inp.size(0); int H = inp.size(2); int W = inp.size(3); int in_ch = inp.size(1); int out_stride = out.stride(0); int in_stride = inp.stride(0); int growth_rate = weight.size(0); const int TILE = 16; const int OC_PER_BLOCK = 8; int tiles_h = (H + TILE - 1) / TILE; int tiles_w = (W + TILE - 1) / TILE; int num_tiles = tiles_h * tiles_w; int num_oc_groups = (growth_rate + OC_PER_BLOCK - 1) / OC_PER_BLOCK; dim3 grid(B, num_tiles, num_oc_groups); dim3 block(TILE * TILE); size_t smem_size = ((TILE + 3) * (TILE + 3) + OC_PER_BLOCK * 9) * sizeof(float); Now let me compile and test correctness. Wait, one issue: when I do `out[:, :current_end]`, this creates a view of `out`. Then `bn(inp)` produces a new tensor `normalized`. Then `layer[1](normalized)` (ReLU inplace) modifies `normalized`. Then I pass `activated` (which is `normalized` after ReLU) to the kernel. The kernel reads from `activated` and writes to `out`. These don't overlap. All good. However, `layer[1]` is `nn.ReLU(inplace=True)`. When called on `normalized` which is a new tensor, it modifies it in place. This is fine and memory-efficient. One subtlety: `normalized = bn(inp)` might not be contiguous. BatchNorm outputs are typically contiguous, but to be safe, should I call `.contiguous()`? PyTorch's BatchNorm2d should produce a contiguous output for contiguous input. Since `out[:, :current_end]` is a view of contiguous `out`, and BatchNorm2d likely outputs a new contiguous tensor, `normalized` should be contiguous. I'll rely on this. Also, `activated` is modified in place from `normalized`, so it's also contiguous. For the kernel, `in_stride = inp.stride(0)`. For a contiguous tensor, this is `in_ch * H * W`. Actually, I just want to verify: after `bn(inp)`, is the output contiguous? Let me check with a quick mental test. `nn.BatchNorm2d` with `track_running_stats=True` (default) does: ```python mean = input.mean([0, 2, 3]) var = input.var([0, 2, 3], unbiased=False) ... return (input - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias ``` The result is computed element-wise, so it should be contiguous if the input is contiguous or using standard tensor ops.
